Abstract 
Inspired by the foundational works in [7] and [3], we introduce a cate- 
gorical framework to formalize Bayesian inference and learning. The two 
key ideas at play here are the notions of Bayesian inversions and the func- 
tor GL as constructed in [3, Â§2.1]. We i nd that Bayesian learning is the 
simplest case of the learning paradigm described in [3]. We then obtain 
categorical formulations of batch and sequential Bayes updates while also 
verifying that the two coincide in a specif i c example.

tween two variables or random vectors. Suppose x is a random vector and y 
is a random variable dependent on x. The additive model assumes that there 
exists a function f such that y = f(x) + Ç« 
where Ç« is a random variable with mean 0. Our goal then reduces to estimating 
the function f. To this end, we consider a parametrized family of functions 
{fÎ¸}Î¸âPwhere P is the parameter space and by means of a learning algorithm 
and a training data set, we traverse P to i nd a candidate that we believe will 
provide a reasonable approximation to the true function f. Several theoreti- 
cal results underpin the validity of this approach- for instance the Universal 
Approximation Theorem.1 / 1ChatGPTThe paper "A Category Theory Framework for Bayesian Learning" by Kotaro Kamiya and John Welliaveetil introduces a categorical framework to formalize Bayesian inference and learning. A standard problem in Machine learning is to understand the relationship be- 
tween two variables or random vectors. Suppose x is a random vector and y 
is a random variable dependent on x. The additive model assumes that there 
exists a function f such that y = f(x) + Ç« 
where Ç« is a random variable with mean 0. Our goal then reduces to estimating 
the function f. To this end, we consider a parametrized family of functions 
{fÎ¸}Î¸âPwhere P is the parameter space and by means of a learning algorithm 
and a training data set, we traverse P to i nd a candidate that we believe will 
provide a reasonable approximation to the true function f. Several theoreti- 
cal results underpin the validity of this approach- for instance the Universal 
Approximation Theorem. 
A formulation of the above framework within Category theory was achieved 
in the foundational work of Fong, Spivak and Tuyeras [7]. In this paper, the 
authors introduce the category Learn whose objects are sets and a morphism 
from A to B in Learn consists of the following data.A parameter set M, 
an implementation I: M Ã A â B, an update function u: M Ã A Ã B â M 
and a request function r: M Ã A Ã B â A. In related work, the authors of 
[3], develop a more general theory by introducing the notion of a Cartesian 
Reverse Dif f erential category (cf. [3, Â§2.2]). Associated to a CRDC- C, they 
construct the analog of the category Learn as a composite of the Para and 
Lens constructions (cf. [3, Â§2.1, 2.3]). In this setting, the learning algorithm 
will be a functor of type Para(C) â Para(Lens(C)). Our goal in this paper is 
to employ both these approaches to develop a categorical framework to discuss 
Bayesian learning. 
While the approach outlined above to model the relationship between two 
random variables is ef f ective, in real world situations, it is often the case that 
there is no such function f.Given the noisy nature of the data, it is more 
reasonable to model the conditional probability p(y|x) i.e. to ask the question - âGiven x, what is the probability that we have y ?â. 
As before, we model the conditional probability using a parametrized family 
of distributions- p(y|x;Î¸). However in contrast to our approach from before, 
we assume that Î¸ is a random variable and strive to obtain a distribution over 
Î¸ that agrees with the given training data. More precisely, we choose a prior 
distribution q(Î¸) on Î¸ and update this prior to obtain the posterior distribution 
on Î¸. As opposed to gradient based approaches to learning, Bayesian machine 
learning updates the prior distribution on Î¸ by exploiting Bayes Theorem. The 
posterior distribution is def i ned upto normalizing constant by the formula 
p(Î¸|y,x) â p(y|x,Î¸)q(Î¸|x). 
There is considerable l exibility in this approach in that we can use the posterior 
to obtain point estimates of the parameter Î¸ via the MAP estimate and perform 
inference by integrating over the entire distribution. We continue this discussion 
in greater detail in Â§2.1 where we illustrate the essential features of Bayesian 
learning in the classical context using a simple example. 
Our goal in this paper is to introduce a framework within category theory 
that allows us to formalize this set up. We draw on the theory already developed 
2 
in [8], [9] and [2]. While we proceed as in [3] by associating to a parametrized 
function a morphism in a generalized lens category which allows for the back- 
ward transmission of information, an important observation is that the Bayes 
Learning framework simplif i es the situation drastically. This is essentially due 
to the fact that with the correct setup, Bayesian inversion is a well def i ned dag- 
ger functor and the functor Copâ Cat responsible for def i ning the generalized 
lens breaks down. We interpret this to mean that Bayes Learning is the sim- 
plest form of learning that adheres to the framework discussed in [3]. See also

of Markov categories as a suitable framework within which one can discuss 
ideas from Probability theory such as Bayesian inversion, disintegration, join- 
tif i cation, conditionalization... Hence, let C be a Markov category. We use the 
categories FinStoch and Stoch (cf. Example 2.3) as guiding examples for the 
theory we develop. Roughly speaking, the category FinStoch has i nite sets as 
objects and Markov kernels as morphisms between them while the objects of 
Stoch are measurable spaces and morphisms f : X â Y imply for every x â X, 
a probability distribution p(Â·|x) on Y . Central to this paper is the notion of 
Bayesian inversion which in the context of FinStoch with reference to a mor- 
phism p: X â Y corresponds to calculating the conditional p(X|y) for y â Y 
using Bayes theorem to obtain a morphism pâ : Y â X. Note that the inversion 
which is a map Y â X is not necessarily unique and in the case of FinStoch 
does not even always exist. To get around this issue, i rstly we restrict our 
attention to those Markov categories C which always admit Bayesian inversion. 
Secondly, we make use of the symmetric monoidal category ProbStoch(C) which 
was introduced i rst by Cho and Jacobs in [2, Â§5] as the category of couplings or 
equivalently the category whose objects coincide with those of the slice category 
I â C and morphisms are obtained by quotienting with respect to the relation of 
almost sure equality. Henceforth, we write PS(C) in place of ProbStoch(C) for 
ease of notation. As a consequence of our assumption on C, Bayesian inversion 
is a well def i ned dagger functor on PS(C). A more detailed explanation can be 
found in Â§2.2. 
We can interpret the set-up in Â§2.1, as a parametrized function in a suitable 
Kleisli category. In general, we assume that the category C is equipped with 
the structure of an M-actegory where M is symmetric monoidal. We would 
like to make use of the ParaMconstruction from [1] which generalizes [7]. For 
reasons explained above, we work with the category PS(C) and in Lemma 3.3, 
we show that PS(C) has the structure of an PS(M)-actegory provided we impose 
certain technical assumptions on M and its action on C. We simplify notation 
henceforth and write PS in place of PS. 
Our def i nition of the functor BayesLearn in Section Â§3 is inspired in large 
part by [3]. The key to the construction of a gradient based learner in [3] is a 
functor GL: Para(E) â Para(Lens(E)) 
where we require that E is a Cartesian reverse dif f erential category. To fully 
describe the learning mechanism, the authors introduce update and error end- 
ofunctors on Para(E). These three pieces when employed in unison and for 
appropriate choices of the update and displacement maps, can describe a wide 
range of optimization algorithms ([3, Â§3.4]). 
3

this end, we proceed as in [9, Def i nition 3.1] by introducing a functor 
Stat: PS(C)opâ Cat

inversion is a well def i ned dagger functor, we deduce the existence of a well 
def i ned functor R: PS(C) â LensStat. 
It follows that we have a functor 
ParaPS(M)(R): ParaPS(M)(PS(C)) â ParaPS(M)(LensStat). 
We refer to the functor ParaPS(M)(R) as the BayesLearn functor.It cap- 
tures the essential features of the Bayes learning algorithm. Indeed, given a 
parametrized morphism X â Y with a state on X, after choosing a prior state 
on the parameter object P, we obtain a morphism in PS(C) i.e. a morphism

2.1Bayesian Inference 
Our goal in this section is to give a high level overview of the approach Bayesian 
learning takes in modelling the relationship between random variables of inter- 
est. We use the lecture [11] as a reference to the material in this section. 
Let us illustrate the approach with an example. Let x = (x1,...,xn) be a 
random vector and y be a random variable. We assume that y is related to x 
via the equation y = f(x) + Ç«

One can divide the Bayesian approach into two parts. As outlined in the intro- 
duction we treat Î² as a random variable and choose a prior distribution which 
encodes our pre-conceived beliefs about Î². Then using the set T, we update the 
distribution on Î² to get its posterior distribution by applying Bayesâ rule. The 
posterior distribution can then be used to obtain point estimates of Î². 
Let us assume an improper prior on Î² i.e. q(Î²) = 1. Applying Bayesâ rule 
gives p(Î²|T) â p(T|Î²)q(Î²)

In this section we outline the construction of the functor BayesLearn which 
aims to capture the essential features of Bayesian learning. However in order 
for us to discuss these results, we require certain preliminary ideas which allow 
us to better understand the actegory structure on PS(C) where C is a Markov 
category. 
3.1Inducing the M-actegory structure

Let's break down the idea of fitting Bayesian theory into category theory in simpler terms:Bayesian Theory: In Bayesian theory, we're interested in understanding the relationship between random variables. We often want to estimate the probability of one variable given another. For instance, given certain data, we want to find the likelihood of an event happening.Category Theory: Category theory is a way of organizing and studying different mathematical structures and their relationships. It provides a high-level framework to understand how different objects (like sets or spaces) relate to each other through morphisms (functions or mappings).Fitting Bayesian Theory into Category Theory: Imagine you have a system where you're trying to learn about the relationship between different variables. Instead of just using traditional probability calculations, you're using the principles of category theory to organize your understanding.In this approach, you use category theory to represent things like random variables, their relationships, and the way you update your understanding with new data. You map the concepts from Bayesian theory into the structures provided by category theory.For example, you can think of the different types of variables as objects in a category, and the relationships between them as morphisms. The process of updating your beliefs with new data can be represented using category theory concepts.Overall, fitting Bayesian theory into category theory is about leveraging the organizational power of category theory to represent and reason about the probabilistic relationships between variables in a more structured and systematic way.1 / 1UNIVERSITY OF CALIFORNIA,

an algorithm for inference over general Riemannian manifolds and is applied to inference on

1.1Geometry in statistics 
It has somehow become the case that humans are capable of spatial reasoning. Our spa- 
tial faculties aid us as we explore and manipulate our environment. These faculties help 
us organize and dichotomize the phenomena we encounter and, somehow, deduce complex 
relationships between these objects. Human induction, however it is accomplished, aids us as 
we project power over nature and ourselves. Given this very primal usefulness, it is not sur- 
prising that geometryâthe mathematical formalization of our spatial intuitionâhas played 
an important role in the development of statistics, the mathematical science of inference, 
whatever that may be. 
Statisticians often assume that the observed data lie in a certain space. A classic example 
is the use of Euclidean geometry in linear regression [151]. Here, the association between 
an outcome variable and explanatory variables is modelled by some line-of-best-f i t. When 
this line is found by minimizing the squared-error between the observed outcomes and i tted 
1 
values, the solution is equivalent to projecting the vector of outcomes onto the linear subspace 
spanned by the covariate vectors. 
Indeed, although data may be high-dimensional and complex in nature, it is often reason- 
able to assume that data points concentrate on some low-dimensional space: Euclidean 
geometry is also used in linear dimensionality reduction (e.g., factor analysis, principal com- 
ponent analysis [140, 103], canonical correlation analysis [98], etc.). Here, the observed, 
high-dimensional data is assumed to have low-dimensional structure that is projected onto 
higher dimensions by some linear transformation. More on linear dimensionality reduction 
and its Bayesian realizations may be found in Chapter 6. 
But there is no reason a priori to assume that the data belongs to a linear subspace of the 
observed support. Manifold learning [56, 172, 14] is a large and active body of research 
based on what is called the âmanifold hypothesisâ [133, 58], the idea that high-dimensional 
data concentrates on low-dimensional, smooth subspaces of the data space called manifolds. 
Spheres and tori are manifolds, but so are amoebae and gently rolled-up pieces of paper. 
Thus, manifold learning requires less stringent assumptions than linear dimensionality re- 
duction and is therefore more challenging. Spectral methods based on the weighted graph 
Laplacian are prime examples of this approach [13]. Although the methodology presented 
in this dissertation leverages manifold (i.e., non-Euclidean) structure, it does not belong to 
the broader class of manifold learning for reasons made explicit in the following paragraphs. 
One may distinguish between the data space and the model space.The previous para- 
graphs feature methods (linear dimensionality reduction, manifold learning) that make as- 
sumptions about the geometry of the data space (although the example of linear regression 
should suggest how this dichotomy might fall apart). On the other hand, likelihood based 
methodologiesâbe they frequentist or Bayesianâprovide geometry further inroads to statis- 
tics. The Fisher information [60, 126] is the expected value of the negative log-likelihood 
Hessian with respect to the data generating distribution specif i ed by the likelihood itself.1 / 1ChatGPTIntroduction:
1.1 Geometry in StatisticsHuman beings possess a natural ability for spatial reasoning, allowing us to navigate and comprehend our surroundings. This innate skill assists us in organizing and understanding the world, enabling us to deduce intricate relationships among objects. This capacity for spatial reasoning plays a pivotal role in induction, empowering us to exert control over nature and our environment. Given its primal significance, it's unsurprising that geometry, which formalizes our spatial intuition mathematically, has found a significant place in statisticsâa mathematical discipline concerned with inference.In statistical analysis, researchers often assume that the data they observe resides within a specific space. A classic instance is the utilization of Euclidean geometry in linear regression. This involves modeling the relationship between an outcome variable and explanatory variables as a line of best fit. Minimizing the squared error between observed outcomes and the fitted values is equivalent to projecting the outcome vector onto the linear subspace spanned by the covariate vectors.While data may often be complex and high-dimensional, it's reasonable to assume that data points tend to concentrate within a lower-dimensional space. This assumption underpins linear dimensionality reduction methods like factor analysis, principal component analysis, and canonical correlation analysis. These techniques project the observed high-dimensional data onto a lower-dimensional space using linear transformations. More on this topic, along with its Bayesian interpretations, is explored in Chapter 6.However, there's no inherent reason to assume a priori that data belongs exclusively to a linear subspace of the observed support. This insight has led to manifold learning, a vibrant field of research based on the "manifold hypothesis." This hypothesis posits that high-dimensional data tends to concentrate within smooth, lower-dimensional subspaces called manifolds. Manifolds can range from spheres and tori to more intricate shapes like amoebae or gently crumpled paper. Manifold learning, unlike linear dimensionality reduction, necessitates less restrictive assumptions, making it more intricate. Spectral methods involving weighted graph Laplacians exemplify this approach. Although the methodology presented in this dissertation leverages non-Euclidean manifold structures, it diverges from broader manifold learning due to specific reasons that will become evident in the subsequent discussion.Differentiating between the data space and the model space is essential. The preceding paragraphs introduced methods that focus on assumptions about the geometry of the data space (although linear regression demonstrates how this distinction can blur). In contrast, likelihood-based methodologiesâwhether frequentist or Bayesianâextend the role of geometry in statistics. The Fisher information is a prime example, representing the expected value of the Hessian matrix of the negative log-likelihood with respect to the data-generating distribution specified by the likelihood function itself.1 / 1The resulting positive def i nite matrix has uses in many areas of statistics, but it may also 
be thought of as an inner product that induces a non-Euclidean geometry on the param- 
eter space and thus the model space. That is, although the parameter space may be the 
Euclidean upper-half plane (as is the case for the univariate Gaussian distribution), the ge- 
ometry induced by the Fisher information matrix is no longer Euclidean. In fact, for the 
Gaussian distribution, the Fisher geometry is equivalent to the hyperbolic half plane model. 
The study of the Fisher geometry is called information geometry [4] and has been studied 
extensively. Chapter 5 of this dissertation discusses the Fisher geometry in depth and relates 
this geometry to the problem of nonparametric density estimation. 
But this dissertation takes a dif f erent approach to model geometry. Whereas information 
geometry begins with Euclidean models and renders them non-Euclidean, I consider models 
for which the parameter space is a priori a non-Euclidean manifold. Covariance matrix mod- 
eling is performed over the manifold of positive def i nite matrices, nonparametric probability 
density functions are naturally identif i ed with points on the inf i nite sphere, and linear sub- 
spaces (over which data vary) are modeled on the Stiefel manifold of orthonormal matrices. 
I argue that, in each of these cases, the natural model geometry provides fresh perspective 
on the modeling task. 
The geometric approach brings computational challenges, so two new algorithms are de- 
veloped in this dissertation. The i rst, geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo (Chapter 2), is 
a Hamiltonian Monte Carlo algorithm for computing posterior distributions def i ned on in- 
trinsically def i ned Riemannian manifolds (see below). In Chapter 4, a new derivation for 
geodesic Monte Carlo on embedded manifolds is presented. This new algorithm extends 
its predecessor and is conceptually simpler. Embedding geodesic Monte Carlo is applied to 
density estimation (Chapter 5) and dimensionality reduction (Chapter 6). 
The chief contents of this dissertation are as follows. In Chapter 2, Bayesian inference is 
performed over the Riemannian manifold of positive def i nite matrices using geodesic La- 
3 
grangian Monte Carlo. The methodology is used for Bayesian spectral density estimation 
and is applied to the analysis of neural time series. In Chapter 3, the derivative of the pseudo 
determinant is derived and used to solve the maximum likelihood problem for the degen- 
erate normal distribution. This derivative is also used in Chapter 4. In this chapter, the 
embedding geodesic Monte Carlo algorithm of [25] is re-derived and extended. In Chapter 5, 
a model is proposed for Bayesian density estimation using the inf i nite-dimensional sphere as 
model space. Finally, in Chapter 6, Bayesian models for linear dimensionality reduction are 
developed for which the model space is the Stiefel manifold, i.e. the space of orthonormal 
matrices of a given dimension. These models are used to decode local i eld potentials and 
high-dimensional neural spike train data generated in a rat hippocampus. 
The proposed model geometric approach intersects with the data geometric and information 
geometric approaches discussed above.In Chapter 6, the orthonormal matrices selected 
by Bayesian inference actually correspond to low-dimensional linear subspaces of the data 
space. This chapter thus serves to undercut the data space, model space dichotomy. Both 
Chapters 2 and 5 touch on the Fisher geometry: the Riemannian structure over the manifold 
of positive def i nite matrices used in Chapter 2 is equivalent to the geometry induced by the 
Fisher metric on the same manifold; in Chapter 5, the geometry of the inf i nite dimensional1 / 1ChatGPTThe resulting positive definite matrix finds application across various statistical domains. However, it also serves as an inner product, giving rise to a non-Euclidean geometry within the parameter and model spaces. While the parameter space could initially be a Euclidean upper-half plane (as in the case of the univariate Gaussian distribution), the Fisher information matrix induces a non-Euclidean geometry. Intriguingly, for the Gaussian distribution, this Fisher geometry aligns with the hyperbolic half-plane model. The study of this geometry is known as information geometry and is explored extensively.Chapter 5 of this dissertation delves deeply into the Fisher geometry and its connection to the challenge of nonparametric density estimation. However, this dissertation takes a distinct approach to model geometry. Rather than starting with Euclidean models and transitioning them into non-Euclidean ones as in information geometry, I focus on models where the parameter space inherently exists as a non-Euclidean manifold. For instance, modeling covariance matrices occurs within the positive definite matrix manifold, while nonparametric probability density functions naturally map onto points on the infinite sphere. Furthermore, linear subspacesâwhere data exhibit variationâare modeled using the Stiefel manifold of orthonormal matrices. This approach, I argue, offers a unique perspective on the modeling task in each case.The geometric approach introduces computational complexities, leading to the development of two novel algorithms within this dissertation. Chapter 4 presents a new derivation of geodesic Monte Carlo on embedded manifolds, expanding on its predecessor and simplifying its conceptual underpinnings. This embedding geodesic Monte Carlo is employed in density estimation (Chapter 5) and dimensionality reduction (Chapter 6).The central contents of this dissertation are as follows: In Chapter 2, Bayesian inference is conducted on the Riemannian manifold of positive definite matrices through geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo. These models are employed in decoding local field potentials and high-dimensional neural spike train data in a rat hippocampus.This novel model geometric approach intersects with the data geometric and information geometric approaches discussed earlier. In this chapter1, we introduce geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo (gLMC), a methodology 
for Bayesian inference on a broad class of Riemannian manifolds. We illustrate this general 
methodology using the space of positive def i nite (PD) matrices as a concrete example. The 
resulting algorithms allow for direct inference on the space of PD matrices and are thus the 
i rst of their kind. As a result, gLMC facilitates better prior elicitation of covariance matrices 
by negating the need for conjugate priors and avoiding dif i cult-to-interpret transformations 
on variables of interest. 
In statistics, positive def i nite matrices primarily appear as covariance matrices parameteriz- 
ing the multivariate Gaussian model. This model is the workhorse of modern statistics and 
machine learning: linear regression, probabilistic principal components analysis, Gaussian 
Markov random i elds, spectral density estimation, and Gaussian process models all rely on 
the multivariate Gaussian distribution. The d-dimensional Gaussian distribution is com- 
pletely specif i ed by a mean vector Âµ and a covariance matrix Î£ in S+ 
d , the space of d-by-d 
PD matrices. By imposing dif f erent structures on the covariance matrix, one can create 
dif f erent models. In some cases, it is possible to parameterize the covariance matrices in 
terms of a small number of parameters. However, learning of the unstructured covariance 
matrices, usually involved in inference on a large number of parameters, has remained as an 
issue. The conjugate Gaussian inverse-Wishart model has known def i ciencies [168]. Outside 
of non-linear parameterizations of the Cholesky decomposition or matrix logarithm, there 
1This chapter is adapted from the paper Geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo over the space of positive 
def i nite matrices: with application to Bayesian spectral density estimation [93], written by A Holbrook,

the Journal of Statistical Computation and Simulation ( c ? 2017 Taylor & Francis).AH

has not yet been a way to perform Bayesian inference directly on the space of PD matrices 
with l exible prior specif i cations using unstructured covariance matrices. 
In this most general context the dif i culty is in sampling from a posterior distribution on an 
abstract, high-dimensional manifold with boundary. It has not been clear how to propose 
moves from point to point within (and without leaving) this space.Our method takes 
advantage of the intrinsic, Riemannian geometry on the space of PD matrices. This space 
is incomplete under the Euclidean metric: following a straight trajectory will often result in 
matrices that are no longer positive def i nite. The space is, however, geodesically complete 
under the canonical metric: no matter how far the sampler travels along any geodesic, 
it never leaves the space of PD matrices. Intuitively, we redef i ne âstraight lineâ in a way 
that precludes leaving the set of interest. Moreover, the metric-induced geodesics provide a 
natural way to traverse the space of PD matrices, and these geodesics i t nicely with recent 
advances in Hamiltonian Monte Carlo on manifolds [66, 25, 112]. 
To this end, we use geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo (gLMC), which belongs to a growing 
class of Hamiltonian Monte Carlo algorithms. Hamiltonian Monte Carlo (HMC) provides 
an intelligent, partially deterministic method for moving around the parameter space while 
leaving the target distribution invariant. New Markov states are generated by numerically integrating a Hamiltonian system while Metropolis-Hastings steps account for the numerical 
error [137]. Riemannian manifold Hamiltonian Monte Carlo (RMHMC) adapts the proposal 
path by incorporating second-order information in the form of a Riemannian metric tensor 
[66]. Lagrangian Monte Carlo (LMC) builds on RMHMC by using a random velocity in 
place of RMHMCâs random momentum.LMCâs explicit integrator is no longer volume 
preserving; it therefore requires Jacobian corrections for each accept-reject step [111]. The 
embedding geodesic Monte Carlo (egMC) [25] is able to take the geometry of the parameter 
space into account while avoiding implicit integration by splitting the Hamiltonian [153] 
into a Euclidean and a geodesic component. Unfortunately, egMC is not applicable when a 
18 
manifoldâs Riemannian embedding is unknown. gLMC, on the other hand, ef i ciently uses 
the same split Hamiltonian formulation as egMC but does not require an explicit Riemannian 
embedding [See 112, for example]. This last fact makes gLMC an ideal candidate for Bayesian 
inference on the space of PD matrices. 
gLMC allows us to treat the entire covariance matrix as one would treat any other model 
parameters. We are no longer restricted to use a conjugate prior distribution or to specify a 
low-rank structure. We illustrate applications of gLMC for PD matrices using both simulated 
and real-world data. First, we show that gLMC provides the same empirical results as the 
closed-form solution for the conjugate Gaussian inverse-Wishart model. After this, we focus 
on applying gLMC for Hermitian PD matrices to multivariate spectral density estimation 
and compare the results obtained from two dif f erent prior specif i cations: the inverse-Wishart 
prior and the complex reference prior. Then, we obtain credible intervals for the squared 
coherences (see Section 2.2) of simulated vector auto-regressive time series for which the 
spectral density matrix is known. Finally, we apply gLMC to learn the spectral density 
matrix associated with multivariate local i eld potentials (LFPs).1 / 1ChatGPT2.1 IntroductionIn this chapter, we introduce geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo (gLMC), a methodology for Bayesian inference on a wide range of Riemannian manifolds. We employ the space of positive definite (PD) matrices as a concrete example to illustrate this approach. The resulting algorithms enable direct inference on the PD matrix space, marking a pioneering effort in this direction. gLMC facilitates more informative prior specification for covariance matrices, eliminating the need for conjugate priors and complex transformations on variables.Positive definite matrices are frequently encountered in statistics as covariance matrices that parameterize the multivariate Gaussian model. This model forms the basis of modern statistics and machine learning, impacting various areas such as linear regression, probabilistic principal component analysis, Gaussian Markov random fields, spectral density estimation, and Gaussian process models. The d-dimensional Gaussian distribution is entirely defined by a mean vector Âµ and a covariance matrix Î£ within S+d, the space of d-by-d positive definite matrices. By introducing various structures to the covariance matrix, diverse models can be constructed. While some cases permit parameterizing covariance matrices using a few parameters, the learning of unstructured covariance matrices, crucial for inference with a large number of parameters, remains challenging. Conventional approaches like the conjugate Gaussian inverse-Wishart model have limitations. Other techniques involve non-linear parameterizations, but a means to perform direct Bayesian inference on the space of positive definite matrices using flexible prior specifications remains absent.The central difficulty lies in sampling from a posterior distribution on a high-dimensional manifold with a boundary. To address this, our method capitalizes on the intrinsic Riemannian geometry of the PD matrix space. This space is incomplete under the Euclidean metric, where moving along a straight path may lead to non-positive definite matrices. However, the space is geodesically complete under the canonical metric. Regardless of how far the sampler traverses along any geodesic, it never departs from the PD matrix space. This redefined notion of a "straight line" ensures that sampling remains within the desired set. The metric-defined geodesics offer a natural way to navigate the PD matrix space, aligning well with recent advancements in Hamiltonian Monte Carlo on manifolds.Geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo (gLMC) is employed for Bayesian inference, belonging to the class of Hamiltonian Monte Carlo algorithms. This methodology integrates numerical integration of a Hamiltonian system with Metropolis-Hastings steps to maintain the invariance of the target distribution. It builds on Riemannian manifold Hamiltonian Monte Carlo (RMHMC), which utilizes second-order information through a Riemannian metric tensor. Lagrangian Monte Carlo (LMC) extends RMHMC by incorporating random velocity instead of random momentum, but requires Jacobian corrections. Embedding geodesic Monte Carlo (egMC) considers parameter space geometry but is inapplicable when the Riemannian embedding of a manifold is unknown. gLMC, however, uses the split Hamiltonian formulation without necessitating an explicit Riemannian embedding, making it ideal for Bayesian inference on the space of PD matrices.gLMC enables the treatment of covariance matrices akin to other model parameters, eliminating the need for conjugate priors or low-rank structures. Its applications for PD matrices are demonstrated using simulated and real-world data. First, gLMC's empirical outcomes align with the closed-form solution of the conjugate Gaussian inverse-Wishart model. Then, the application of gLMC to Hermitian PD matrices for multivariate spectral density estimation is highlighted. We compare results from different prior specifications, including the inverse-Wishart prior and complex reference prior. Credible intervals for squared coherences are obtained for simulated vector auto-regressive time series with known spectral density matrices. Lastly, gLMC is used to learn spectral density matrices associated with multivariate local field potentials (LFPs).1 / 1The contributions of this chapter are as follows: 
â¢ gLMC, an MCMC methodology for Bayesian inference on general Riemannian mani- 
folds, is proposed; 
â¢ to illustrate the general methodology, we provide a detailed description of gLMC on 
the spaces of symmetric and Hermitian PD matrices; 
â¢ for classical statisticians, the chapter serves as a brief introduction to spectral density 
estimation and its Bayesian approach; 
â¢ the proposed algorithms are applied to Bayesian inference on (real and complex) covari- 
ance matrices based on simulated and real-world data and using a number of dif f erent

It should be noted that the proposed method is useful for generating samples from the 
posterior distribution of interest and not just a point estimate. The proposed method is 
for full inference of a posterior distribution def i ned directly over the space of PD matrices 
without limiting ourselves to conjugate priors, as such, is the i rst of its kind. 
That said, it is sometimes suf i cient for the scientist to obtain a point estimate of the co- 
variance or spectral density matrix. In this context, regularization of the estimate is often 
advantageous. Regularization approaches may be interpreted as Bayesian and their corre- 
sponding point estimates are interpreted as maximum a posteriori (MAP) estimates. See 
[144] for a statistically minded survey of covariance estimation and regularization, and see 
[1] for a state-of-science approach to point estimation in signal processing applications. 
The rest of the chapter is outlined thus: in Section 2.2, we provide motivation for our 
approach in the form of a brief introduction to spectral density estimation for multivariate 
time series; in Section 2.3, we def i ne PD matrices and show how the space of PD (symmetric 
or Hermitian) matrices comprises a Riemannian manifold; in Section 2.4, we present the 
gLMC methodology for Bayesian inference on general Riemannian manifolds; in Section 2.5, 
we detail gLMC on the spaces of symmetric and Hermitian PD matrices; in Section 2.6, 
we introduce the reader to common proper and improper priors for covariance matrices; in 
Section 2.7, we present empirical results based on simulated and real-world data.1 / 1ChatGPTContributions of this ChapterThis chapter makes several significant contributions:gLMC Methodology: The chapter introduces the geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo (gLMC) methodology, providing a novel approach for Bayesian inference on general Riemannian manifolds.Detailed Description of gLMC: The general methodology is illustrated through a detailed explanation of how gLMC operates on the spaces of symmetric and Hermitian positive definite (PD) matrices.Introduction to Spectral Density Estimation: For classical statisticians, the chapter serves as a brief introduction to spectral density estimation and its Bayesian approach.Application to Covariance Matrices: The proposed algorithms are applied to Bayesian inference on both real and complex covariance matrices, using various prior specifications and simulated/real-world data.Direct Inference: The proposed method is valuable for generating samples from the full posterior distribution, rather than just a point estimate. Bayesian inference is hard. Bayesian inference on non-Euclidean manifolds is harder. Prior 
to the publication of [25], a statistician required great ingenuity to compute the posterior 
distribution for any model with non-Euclidean parameter space, and the algorithmic details 
might change signif i cantly depending on the prior, the likelihood, and the constraints implied 
by the non-Euclidean geometry. A good example of this approach is found in [88], where 
the posterior distribution over the Stiefel manifold of orthonormal matrices is computed by 
way of column-at-a-time Gibbs updates that rely on model specif i cations. 
It is preferable, rather, that the same algorithm work for many dif f erent kinds of models. 
This is one of the strengths of Hamiltonian Monte Carlo [49] and its Riemannian extension, 
RMHMC [66], which augments the posterior distribution Ï(q) by the random Gaussian

by simulating the augmented canonical distribution with Hamiltonian

Prior to the publication of [25], performing posterior computations for models with non-Euclidean parameter spaces required significant ingenuity. The algorithmic approach often varied based on factors such as the prior, the likelihood, and the constraints imposed by the non-Euclidean geometry. 4.4Discussion 
We have proposed an alternative derivation to the geodesic Monte Carlo for embedded 
manifolds [25]. This derivation is conceptually simpler, as it does not rely on a notion of 
intrinsic manifold geometry and thus clarif i es the algorithm. Specif i cally, it becomes clear 
that the inclusion of the log-Jacobian of the embedding in the Hamiltonian is unnecessary 
in any case where the target distribution is def i ned with respect to embedding coordinates. 
The resulting class of algorithms are symplectic and completely explicit (do not require 
implicit integration). The algorithm also allows for non-trivial mass matrix as an added 
73 
benef i t. Finally, the exposition hints how Metropolis adjustments may be incorporated into 
geometric Langevin algorithms such as [117].1 / 1ChatGPTDiscussion on Embedding Geodesic Monte CarloAn alternative derivation of the embedding geodesic Monte Carlo for embedded manifolds, as proposed in [25], has been introduced. This derivation is characterized by its conceptual simplicity, as it eliminates the reliance on intrinsic manifold geometry notions, making the algorithm's mechanics clearer. In this approach, it becomes evident that incorporating the log-Jacobian of the embedding into the Hamiltonian is unnecessary whenever the target distribution is defined in terms of embedding coordinates.The resulting class of algorithms is both symplectic and entirely explicit, eliminating the need for implicit integration. Moreover, these algorithms permit the use of a non-trivial mass matrix, offering an additional advantage. This approach also suggests the possibility of integrating Metropolis adjustments into geometric Langevin algorithms, similar to the concepts presented in [117].1 / 1Chapter Summary 
It is well known that the Fisher information induces a Riemannian geometry on para- 
metric families of probability density functions. Following recent work, we consider 
the nonparametric generalization of the Fisher geometry. The resulting nonparametric 
Fisher geometry is shown to be equivalent to a familiar, albeit inf i nite-dimensional, ge- 
ometric objectâthe sphere. By shifting focus away from density functions and toward 
square-root density functions, one may calculate theoretical quantities of interest with 
ease. More importantly, the sphere of square-root densities is much more computation- 
ally tractable. This insight leads to a novel Bayesian nonparametric density estimation 
model. We construct the Ï2-process density prior by modeling the square-root density 
with a restricted Gaussian process prior. Inference over square-root densities is fast, 
and the model retains the l exibility characteristic of Bayesian nonparametric mod- 
els. Finally, we formalize the relationship between embedding geodesic Monte Carlo 
(egMC) on the inf i nite-dimensional sphere and standard Riemannian HMC. 
76 
5.1Introduction 
The Fisher informationâand the geometry it inducesâhas been one of the unequivocal suc- 
cess stories of geometry in statistics. Building on recent work, we extend the Fisher geometry 
beyond parametric statistical models and show that the resulting geometry is equivalent to 
that of the inf i nite-dimensional sphere. The purpose of this chapter is to bring attention to 
this new perspective and to demonstrate its theoretical and methodological consequences. 
As an application, we introduce the Ï2-process density prior, a l exible nonparametric model 
for Bayesian density estimation that admits fast computation while requiring minimal as- 
sumptions. 
The Fisher information matrix is canonical in statistics: it is rooted in information theory 
[68]; it appears in Jef f reyâs prior of Bayesian analysis [101]; and it plays a central role 
in Bayesian and Frequentist asymptotics [113].Fisher advocated the importance of the 
information matrix in maximum likelihood estimation [60]. Fisherâs student, Rao, was the 
i rst to place the information matrix in a dif f erential geometric context [147]. Since then, the 
dif f erential geometric implications for parametric statistical models have been the subject 
of extensive inquiry [4]. Recently, a number of researchers have drawn connections between 
the Fisher geometry and the geometry of the inf i nite sphere [160, 30, 100, 109, 161, 142]. 
Much of this work has been in the area of shape analysis and has focused on using the 
Fisher geometry to measure distance between probability densities. 1 / 1ChatGPTChapter SummaryThis chapter explores the extension of the Fisher information's Riemannian geometry beyond parametric statistical models to nonparametric cases. The resulting nonparametric Fisher geometry is found to be equivalent to the geometry of the inf i nite-dimensional sphere. This perspective shift offers computational advantages and leads to the development of a novel Bayesian nonparametric density estimation model called the Ï2-process density prior. This prior enables efficient computation while maintaining the flexibility typical of Bayesian nonparametric models. Additionally, the chapter formalizes the relationship between embedding geodesic Monte Carlo (egMC) on the inf i nite-dimensional sphere and standard Riemannian Hamiltonian Monte Carlo. The chapter showcases the theoretical and methodological implications of this new perspective on geometry in statistics.1 / 1Chapter 7 
Future directions: Bayesian inference 
on inf i nite manifolds 
One of the most immediate extensions of the methodology presented in this dissertation is the 
extension from i nite dimensional manifolds to Hilbert manifolds. Many problems in statistics 
and applied mathematics require a solution that belongs to a set of constrained functions. 
Although these functional constraints (e.g. non-negativity, orthogonality, normality, etc.) 
often complicate problems, structure and symmetries on the solution space can be leveraged 
to simplify statistical models and speed-up computations. On the one hand, manifolds are 
a large class of spaces that are useful in many of the mathematical sciences [65, 84]. On the 
other hand, functions are the objects of interest, but spaces of functions are (in most cases) 
inf i nite-dimensional. Future research may demonstrate that manifold structure can provide 
order to a statistical problem, even when the solution space is an inf i nite-dimensional space 
of functions. 
Bayesian inference on functional manifolds necessitates new computational and modelling 
techniques that rely upon the foundations of dif f erential geometry, inverse-problems, and 
130 
Bayesian nonparametrics. Such techniques would allow for scalable applications of Bayesian 
methods to multiple complex spatial and temporal problems. 
In Chapter 5, I used a reinterpretation of information geometry [4] to inspire a new approach 
to Bayesian nonparametric density estimation [92]. Whereas density estimation has tradi- 
tionally focused on functions that are non-negative and integrate to unity, I focused on the 
square-root of this function and thus identif i ed the problem with Bayesian inference on the 
L2sphere

One immediate extension is moving from finite-dimensional manifolds to Hilbert manifolds, which would be particularly useful when dealing with constrained functions, such as those with non-negativity, orthogonality, or other properties. Functional constraints often complicate problems, but utilizing the structure and symmetries of the solution space could simplify statistical models and computational efforts. This involves delving into the realm of dif f erential geometry, inverse problems, and Bayesian nonparametrics to develop techniques for Bayesian inference on infinite-dimensional functional manifolds.The dissertation's approach to Bayesian nonparametric density estimation focused on using the square root of the density function and treating it as a point on the L2 sphere. This led to a Gaussian process (GP) prior multiplied by a Dirac measure, enforcing the constraint on the L2 sphere. This approach eased computational constraints compared to the traditional method of ensuring a function integrates to 1. The chapter emphasizes the potential for utilizing similar techniques to solve problems on infinite manifolds and suggests that this exploration could open up new opportunities for scalable applications of Bayesian methods to complex spatial and temporal problems.1 / 1where x â D denotes position, t denotes time, and Î¨ satisf i es

HMC has facilitated ef i cient computing for state spaces that were formerly dif i cult to 
perform Bayesian inference on: embedding geodesic Monte Carlo [25] (Chapters 4-6) and 
geodesic Lagrangian Monte Carlo (Chapter 2) ef i ciently perform Bayesian inference on many 
i nite dimensional manifolds. In parallel to the development of HMC algorithms for i nite 
manifolds, the HMC class has been extended to compute probability measures on Hilbert 
spaces [19, 37]. This inf i nite-dimensional extension has been helpful for the growth of the 
i eld of Bayesian inverse problems. 
As of today, HMC has not been extended to manifolds of functions. One reason is that such 
an extension has been mostly unmotivated outside the density estimation model of Chapter 
5. Work is needed that builds on i nite manifold HMC algorithms so that it provably and 
ef i ciently scales in the inf i nite-dimensional limit.1 / 1ChatGPTThe provided text discusses the potential application of the proposed methodology to the field of quantum physics and Bayesian inverse problems. It's a revolutionary read that's reshaping our perception of the world around us! ððð§µð#UnlockTheMysteries #GeometricBayesianism1 / 1Geometric Bayesianism: A Distinct Perspective in Bayesian Inference

Geometric Bayesianism introduces a fresh dimension to the landscape of Bayesian inference, infusing the realm of probability and data analysis with geometric concepts. While traditional Bayesian methodologies often navigate abstract probability distributions and their interactions, this approach charts a course through the geometric interpretation of these distributions.

Key Tenets:

Geometry-Meets-Probability:

At its core, Geometric Bayesianism bridges the gap between statistics and geometry. It views probability distributions not merely as abstract mathematical entities, but as vectors in a specific vector space.
This conceptual leap transforms distributions into tangible entities with magnitude and direction, analogous to geometric vectors.
Inner Product Insight:

The heart of this approach lies in the establishment of an inner product within this vector space. This inner product serves as a mathematical tool to measure the angle between different probability distributions.
This angle becomes a meaningful metric of how distinct or similar these distributions are. It encapsulates the idea of compatibility, revealing the alignment or divergence between distributions.
Prior, Likelihood, Posterior:

The interplay between prior, likelihood, and posterior distributions becomes a dynamic interplay of vectors in this geometric framework.
The likelihood functionâs role as an information conveyor gains geometric representation, and the priorâs beliefs transform into a geometric vector.
Cosine of Compatibility:

The standout contribution of Geometric Bayesianism is the concept of compatibility. Expressed as the cosine of the angle between distributions, it quantifies the extent of alignment.
Compatibility values range from zero to one, translating geometric insights into a numerical measure of agreement.
Implications and Exploration:

Visualizing Uncertainty:

Geometric Bayesianism offers an intuitive means to visualize uncertainty. Probability distributions cease to be abstract curves on graphs; they become arrows in space, pointing to possibilities and uncertainties.
Comparing and Contrasting Distributions:

Geometric measures enable the quantitative evaluation of how prior beliefs align with observed data, how likelihood functions influence inferences, and how posterior distributions evolve.
This approach facilitates rigorous comparisons between different prior distributions, shedding light on which beliefs best resonate with data.
Applications Across Fields:

The realm of Geometric Bayesianism isn't confined to a single domain. It extends across fields like machine learning, statistics, and even philosophy.
Its capacity to unravel relationships and unveil shifts in belief systems has implications for diverse disciplines.
In Summation:

Geometric Bayesianism introduces a geometric lens through which the intricate dance of Bayesian inference is perceived. By fusing geometry with probability distributions, it forges a path to quantifiable understanding, rendering complex statistical concepts more tangible and relatable.

Through this unique perspective, Geometric Bayesianism joins the symphony of Bayesian methodologies, offering a harmonious blend of mathematics and geometry that enhances our comprehension of probability and its interactions.1 / 1ChatGPT"Geometric Bayesianism: A Distinct Perspective in Bayesian Inference"Geometric Bayesianism is a novel approach that injects geometric concepts into the realm of Bayesian inference, revolutionizing how we understand probability and data analysis. Let's explore the connections between the topics we discussed and the concepts of Curry-Howard isomorphism, the Church-Turing thesis, and the functional equivalence of different programming paradigms:Curry-Howard Isomorphism:
The Curry-Howard isomorphism establishes a deep connection between logic and computation. It links logical proofs with computer programs and types with propositions. In the context of Bayesian learning, this isomorphism can manifest as the correspondence between Bayesian inference processes and the construction of proofs or programs. Just as different logical systems can lead to equivalent proofs, different probabilistic models can lead to equivalent Bayesian inferences.Church-Turing Thesis:
The Church-Turing thesis asserts the equivalence of different computational models in terms of their expressive power. In the context of Bayesian learning, this thesis suggests that different ways of specifying prior beliefs, likelihood functions, and posterior distributions can ultimately lead to the same results. Whether you express Bayesian inference using category theory, geometry, or other formalisms, the outcomes should be equivalent if the models are equivalent.Functional Equivalence of Programming Paradigms:
The functional equivalence of programming paradigms, such as imperative and declarative styles, mirrors the idea that different mathematical formalisms or conceptual frameworks can lead to the same results in Bayesian learning. Just as different programming languages can achieve the same computation using distinct approaches, different mathematical approaches can yield equivalent Bayesian inference outcomes.These connections highlight the overarching principle that different representations or formalisms can capture the same underlying concepts and relationships. Whether in logic, computation, or Bayesian learning, the central ideas remain constant, even as the ways of expressing them may vary. This connects the geometric aspects of simplices with the categorical notion of morphisms between objects.While simplices themselves are not categories, they can be used to construct structures that align with category theory concepts. Its defining method can briefly be described as "going backwards from the theorems to the axioms", in contrast to the ordinary mathematical practice of deriving theorems from axioms. It can be conceptualized as sculpting out necessary conditions from sufficient ones.

The reverse mathematics program was foreshadowed by results in set theory such as the classical theorem that the axiom of choice and Zorn's lemma are equivalent over ZF set theory. The goal of reverse mathematics, however, is to study possible axioms of ordinary theorems of mathematics rather than possible axioms for set theory.1 / 1ChatGPTReverse mathematics is a fascinating program in mathematical logic that takes a distinctive approach to understanding the axiomatic foundations of mathematics. Here's how it connects with the topics we've discussed:Category Theory for Bayesian Learning: While category theory primarily focuses on relationships between mathematical structures, reverse mathematics provides insight into the axioms necessary for proving theorems. The program involves analyzing the logical strength of theorems, which can be analogous to examining the relationships between different mathematical concepts.Geometric Bayesianism: Geometric Bayesianism introduces geometric concepts to Bayesian inference. Similarly, reverse mathematics involves "sculpting out" necessary conditions from sufficient ones. This notion of finding essential components aligns with the way geometric Bayesianism seeks to understand the essential relationships between probability distributions.Functional Equivalence of Programming Paradigms: The idea of seeking necessary conditions aligns with the functional equivalence perspective in programming paradigms. Just as different programming paradigms can achieve the same outcome, reverse mathematics aims to find common axiomatic foundations for theorems that might seem unrelated at first.Church-Turing Thesis: The reverse mathematics program's approach to analyzing axioms resonates with the idea of finding minimal computational requirements for certain tasks, as in the Church-Turing thesis. The program's focus on identifying axioms for theorems can be seen as a quest for the minimal computational or logical structure needed to establish those theorems.Implicit Use of Category Theory: While reverse mathematics might not explicitly involve category theory, the concept of uncovering necessary conditions for theorems has conceptual parallels with category theory's emphasis on relationships between mathematical objects. Both approaches involve identifying core components that contribute to broader mathematical structures.Mathematical Simplices: Reverse mathematics explores the foundational axioms required to prove theorems, which can be seen as identifying the "simplest" building blocks of mathematical reasoning. This idea of breaking down complex theorems into their minimal axiomatic components has a parallel with the role of mathematical simplices as foundational geometric objects.In summary, reverse mathematics provides a different perspective on the axiomatic foundations of mathematics, aligning with concepts of abstraction, minimalism, and necessary conditions that have parallels with the topics we've discussed.1 / 1In reverse mathematics, one starts with a framework language and a base theoryâa core axiom systemâthat is too weak to prove most of the theorems one might be interested in, but still powerful enough to develop the definitions necessary to state these theorems. For example, to study the theorem âEvery bounded sequence of real numbers has a supremumâ it is necessary to use a base system that can speak of real numbers and sequences of real numbers.

For each theorem that can be stated in the base system but is not provable in the base system, the goal is to determine the particular axiom system (stronger than the base system) that is necessary to prove that theorem. To show that a system S is required to prove a theorem T, two proofs are required. The first proof shows T is provable from S; this is an ordinary mathematical proof along with a justification that it can be carried out in the system S. The second proof, known as a reversal, shows that T itself implies S; this proof is carried out in the base system.[1] The reversal establishes that no axiom system Sâ² that extends the base system can be weaker than S while still proving T.1 / 1ChatGPTReverse mathematics is a methodical approach that seeks to uncover the minimal axiomatic requirements needed to prove theorems in mathematics. Let's connect this idea to the concepts we've discussed:Category Theory for Bayesian Learning: Just as category theory provides a way to study relationships between mathematical structures, reverse mathematics focuses on relationships between axioms and theorems. The goal is to understand which axioms are essential to prove specific theorems, similar to how category theory highlights foundational relationships between mathematical concepts.Geometric Bayesianism: Geometric Bayesianism introduces a geometric perspective to Bayesian inference. Similarly, reverse mathematics "sculpts out" the necessary axioms from theorems. This connection can be seen in the process of identifying the minimal set of axioms required to establish a given theorem.Functional Equivalence of Programming Paradigms: The reverse mathematics approach aligns with the functional equivalence perspective in programming paradigms. In both cases, the focus is on finding the core axioms that underlie specific outcomes or theorems. The study of minimal axioms in reverse mathematics can be seen as analogous to finding the minimal set of operations needed to achieve specific computations in programming.Church-Turing Thesis: The reverse mathematics program's aim to identify the minimal axioms necessary for proving theorems resonates with the Church-Turing thesis. Both concepts involve seeking the simplest and most foundational components needed to achieve specific mathematical or computational outcomes.Implicit Use of Category Theory: While not explicitly rooted in category theory, the reverse mathematics approach shares the spirit of analyzing foundational relationships between axioms and theorems. The process of determining the necessary axioms for specific theorems aligns with the overarching theme of uncovering fundamental connections.Mathematical Simplices: Reverse mathematics is about breaking down complex theorems into their essential axiomatic components. This aligns with the role of mathematical simplices as foundational geometric objects that form the building blocks of more complex geometries.In summary, reverse mathematics offers a systematic way to analyze the axiomatic requirements for proving theorems, highlighting the connections between foundational components and broader mathematical structures. This approach finds parallels with the concepts we've discussed, emphasizing the pursuit of minimalism, foundational relationships, and essential conditions.1 / 1I think we are engaging in a kind of reverse mathematics trying to understand what axioms we need from probability theory, category theory, and vector calculus to understand geometric Bayesianism1 / 1ChatGPTAbsolutely, your observation is spot-on! The seeds of Diachronic Reverse Mathematics can be traced back to ancient civilizations where mathematical thinking was intertwined with philosophical inquiries. The Renaissance period witnessed a shift towards rigor and systematization. This period also saw the emergence of the concept of expected value, providing a bridge between probability and mathematics.Philosophical Insights:

The 18th and 19th centuries saw the development of vector calculus, driven by the works of mathematicians like Gauss, Green, and Stokes. The integration of calculus with vector algebra paved the way for the mathematical tools necessary for modern geometric interpretations.Category Theory's Emergence:
Category theory, a branch of mathematics concerned with abstract relationships between mathematical structures, emerged in the mid-20th century. Samuel Eilenberg and Saunders Mac Lane's foundational work on category theory provided a new lens through which to view mathematical concepts and relationships, influencing fields ranging from algebra to logic.Integration and Synthesis:

Ever since Newton created dynamics, there has been controversy about its foundations. Are space and time absolute? Do they form a rigid but invisible framework and container of the universe? Or are space, time, and motion relative? If so, does Newton's 'framework' arise through the influence of the universe at large, as Ernst Mach suggested? Einstein's aim when creating his general theory of relativity was to demonstrate this and thereby implement 'Mach's Principle'. However, it is widely believed that he achieved only partial success. This question of whether motion is absolute or relative has been a central issues in philosophy; the nature of time has perennial interest. Current attempts to create a quantum description of the whole universe keep these issues at the cutting edge of modern research. Written by the world's leading expert on Mach's Principle, The Discovery of Dynamics is a highly original account of the development of notions about space, time, and motion. Widely praised in its hardback version, it is one of the fullest and most readable accounts of the astronomical studies that culminated in Kepler's laws of planetary motion and of the creation of dynamics by Galileo, Descartes, Huygens, and Newton. Originally published as Absolute or Relative Motion?, Vol. 1: The Discovery of Dynamics (Cambridge), The Discovery of Dynamics provides the technical background to Barbour's recently published The End of Time, in which he argues that time disappears from the description of the quantum universe.1 / 1ChatGPT"The Discovery of Dynamics: A Study from a Machian Point of View of the Discovery and the Structure of Dynamical Theories" by Julian B. Summarize: Geometric interpretation of probability distributions
Michael Larionov, PhD
Towards Data Science
Michael Larionov, PhD

Photo by Shubham Dhage on Unsplash
Bayesian inference is based on the fact that we often donât know the underlying distribution of data, so we need to build a model and then iteratively adjust it as we get more data. In parametric Bayesian inference you start with picking a general form of the probability distribution f(x;Î¸) defined by parameters Î¸. A good example of the distribution could be a Normal distribution with two parameters Î¼ and Ï^2. The probability of the data under a hypothetical distribution, assuming independent data examples, is:

This function is called likelihood function. The parameter Î¸ is itself a random variable, and its probability distribution can be found using Bayesâ theorem:

Here p(Î¸) is called posterior distribution, Ï(Î¸) is prior distribution and expresses our beliefs about parameter Î¸ before we see any data. The term in the denominator is called evidence and represents probability of data. It is easier to think of it as a normalizing constant, to make sure p(Î¸) is a valid distribution.

Bayesian Geometry
The idea behind Bayes Geometry is simple: what if we represent any function in the parameter space as a vector in a certain vector space. Examples of these functions could be prior and posterior distributions and likelihood functions. Then we can define an inner product on that space that will help us to calculate an angle between two distributions and interpret the angle as a measure of how much the distributions are different from each other. In my discussion on this subject I will follow a paper by de Carvalho et al[1] as well as this slide deck.

The inner product between two functions in the parameter space is defined as:

Source https://sheffieldmlnet.github.io/downloads/decarvalho-geometric-aspects-bayesian-inference-slides.pdf
and the norm of the function is:

We restrict our choice of functions to the one where the norm exists. This condition is generally not guaranteed as there are some probability distributions for which the norm diverges.

One thing we can immediately see that the evidence, defined above, is inner product between prior distribution and likelihood. Below is a schematic diagram for interaction between prior distribution, likelihood and posterior distribution. A large angle between the prior distribution and the likelihood indicates that the prior distribution has poor data alignment.

Source: https://sheffieldmlnet.github.io/downloads/decarvalho-geometric-aspects-bayesian-inference-slides.pdf
The main contribution in [1] is called compatibility, which can be interpreted as cosine of the angle between two functions in the parameter space:

Source: https://sheffieldmlnet.github.io/downloads/decarvalho-geometric-aspects-bayesian-inference-slides.pdf
Compatibility is a value between zero and one, and can be used to judge how well aligned are prior distribution to likelihood, two prior distributions, two likelihoods, etc. It can be calculated in closed form for some cases, for more complicated distributions it can be computed using Markov Chain Monte Carlo algorithm.

Discussion
There are several areas which I would like to discuss regarding this approach:

The paper defines a Hilbert space on the functions in the parameter space. This means that a linear combination of two functions also belongs to the Hilbert space. Such linear combination is interpreted as a mixture of two probability distributions. However, to become a proper distribution it must be normalized so that the integral over all values of the parameter is one:
Does unnormalized mixture have any meaning? By the way, the likelihood function does not need to be normalized.

2. A bit of confusion can arise regarding the concept of normalization for probability distributions. To be proper it needs to be normalized using the equation above. However Hilbert space adds additional norm, which is integral not of the PDF, but of its square. It is hard for me to understand any meaning of the second norm other than to support the constructions related to the Hilbert space.

3. Likelihood is not an additive, but multiplicative value. If I have a batch of data that has likelihood L1 and another (independent) batch of data with likelihood L2, the4 combined likelihood is a product of L1 and L2, not their sum. Which makes me believe that maybe a better approach is to introduce a vector space in the logarithmic domain or use an algebraic construct more complex than Hilbert space.

References
[1] de Carvalho, M., Page, G. L., & Barney, B. J. (2019). On the geometry of Bayesian inference. Bayesian Analysis, 14(4). https://doi.org/10.1214/18-BA1112

Visual Complex Analysis
Front Cover
Tristan Needham
Clarendon Press, 1997 - Complexes - 592 pages
This radical first course on complex analysis brings a beautiful and powerful subject to life by consistently using geometry (not calculation) as the means of explanation. Aimed at undergraduate students in mathematics, physics, and engineering, the book's intuitive explanations, lack of
advanced prerequisites, and consciously user-friendly prose style will help students to master the subject more readily than was previously possible. The key to this is the book's use of new geometric arguments in place of the standard calculational ones. These geometric arguments are communicated
with the aid of hundreds of diagrams of a standard seldom encountered in mathematical works. A new approach to a classical topic, this work will be of interest to students in mathematics, physics, and engineering, as well as to professionals in these fields.
